I'll put this out there so you can consider it for future leatherwork endeavors: you can buy the dye/paint that automotive upholstery shops use to recondition leather and leatherette seats. It's basically a rubberized acrylic paint specifically designed to bond to leather and vinyl while remaining flexible and compliant. While not exactly designed to be applied with brushes, you can do so if you wish. Just a reminder that you may want to create a silicone cast of some nice leather texture so you can imprint it back into the paint if you apply it too thick. Also as a side note: most automotive leather dye/paint is designed to be applied either with a sponge or with an airbrush, so you can actually airbrush or sponge gradients with it if you buy enough colors.

As someone who does leather (I actually started because of this channel), you can mix multiple dyes together in a separate cup to get a color in the middle. You can also add just isopropyl alcohol to make it lighter. Also, alcohol markers work really well on leather if you are doing smaller sections/items. There's a good chance Martina already figured this out, but I thought I would share what I have learned.   Also leather paint is basically just watered down acrylic paint.

If I'd had the opportunity to apprentice under someone, even for about a year, just to learn beautiful crafts like this, I'm sure I would've done it. But we didn't know anyone like this. Nobody I knew knew anyone like y'all. If you were a nerd or a creator, you kept it to yourself. Leatherworking, bookbinding--we have machines for that now; such trades are as good as obsolete. Those who do them "weren't good enough" for a "real" education (i.e., university degree). But it's all classist propaganda, & I'm stuck making my mistakes, watching YouTube to learn how to make things that make me & others like me happy. I love this. I want to bind books. I learned so much from just this one video. Thanks a ton. Keep up the good work.

As someone who used to work in a bookbinding department of a local printworks, the different sizes of book blocks (+/- 1mm or so) can happen for a variety of reasons. Most commonly it's different settings on the folding machines and the three-blade trimmers. Personally I'd go with aligning them on the spine and head of the block. That being said, the end result is absolutely insanely amazing :)

It may be semi-usable to read this by spreading it flat on the table. Lay it lengthwise, spine down. Open the first book. As you amass the pages, open the last book to even it out. Might need support in the middle or maybe the linen can hold it. Once in midway, you have a tall stack, then just let it "slide sideways" as you go to the end, finishing reading with book laying flat on a table. This will make reading of some pages awkward, as they will be vertical, but doable.

As soon I saw the thumbnail, I figured a solution how to read it kinda practically. Basically, you need a table with a oblong hole in it, with a seesaw in the middle. It'll have to be nicely balanced and as you go through the book, the weight on one side will raise the other so that the part that you are reading is level. Also the seesaw should be offset lower that the main plane of the table to accomodate the height of the book. Imagine it as a seesaw with two pistons where the weighted down one raises the lighter one up. You could make a table like that (maybe a collab with some woodworking youtuber?) And then auction/sell/donate or whatever the table with the book.

Just for future reference, if you ever need to trim books down to the same size like that, if you have a local printing company near you, they likely have a large cutting press, you might be able to get them to cut the books to the same size for you.  I worked at my father's company in the summer one year and used one.  They can enter the size needed and then trim all the books to the exact same size.   The one I used was able to cut 1000 sheets at once.

Fun fact: when Robert Jordan started work on what he intended to be the eleventh and final volume of the main series (i.e., not counting _New Spring_), he said that it didn't matter if it turned out to be two thousand pages, it'd be the last one. Unfortunately, he was already quite ill, which is why Brandon Sanderson wound up stepping in at Jordan's wife's request to complete the series in what turned out to be three volumes. So binding together the books co-written by Sanderson would, at least, be consistent with Jordan's intentions ğŸ˜.  (I personally checked out of the series after reading _Crossroads of Twilight_, and I doubt I'll ever go back to read the whole series â€” there's too much other fiction out there I haven't read that excites me way more â€” but I'm not going to yuck anyone's yum by talking about why here.) I don't work with periodicals, but we have a bunch of these bound journals which at times are thicker than they are tall. It's cheaper to order such a thick boi from your bookbinder than to have each individual issue bound, or even a few issues bound together. Fun fact for those curious about vegetable tanning: leather is made a few different ways, but the most common ones are chrome tanning, which involves treating the hides with chromium salts to make them flexible and non-porous, and vegetable tanning, which uses organic compounds called tannins. Yep, the same ones in tea and red wine. They're called that because they tan leather. Vegetable-tanned leather is more durable and much better for tooling and the like, at the cost of being stiffer, more porous, and more expensive. Chrome tanned leather is usually what's used for jackets and furniture, but it doesn't age as well and it's harder to dye after it's left the factory. Also it's an ecological disaster zone, but what isn't these days. Neither of these are the historical method of producing leather, which was much more disgusting, and I'm not gonna go into it. Seriously, don't google it while eating. And yes, if you wanted to, you could bind a book with chrome-tanned leather, but it won't last as long and you can't tool it, so there really isn't a point unless you want your book to look like a biker jacket. This is your fun fact of the day.  TL;DR: tree bark and acorns make leather for books, chromium salts make leather for handbags, and this project was EXPENSIVE.

okay but this is also the perfect canvas for painting on the side of the pages because itâ€™s so huge
Normally to align the pages you'd use a guillotine knife and trim them identically, but these aren't going to cut more than 1500-2000 sheets at a time. Even with a hydraulic clamp (set as heavy as possible without risk of damaging the paper) and a freshly sharpened blade, you'll likely get a millimeter-ish of draw on the cut. And what you have there looks more like 10+ times that. There's no blade sharp enough to trim that much paper without SIGNIFICANT draw.  So maybe you could have used a belt sander or similar? While the whole book is clamped, just sand down the sides to smooth them out. The challenge here would be to get them perfectly parallel and aligned. Mind you, I'd do a test first because who knows if that would just look like poo.

I'm a professionnal bookbinder who graduated from Ã©cole Estienne and I must say this is some great stuff, I can't wait for this monstrosity to be seen in a few decades or centuries in a rare book fair. Us bookbinders love funky shapes and systems. This is for sure a great fit. I'm pretty sure we all asked ourselves at some point "what if the text block was much wider than the spine is long ?" and this is a perfect example. Also this act of binding together multiple books is not new, it's called Sammelband (and other names but this one will work for an international comment on a youtube video) and has great historical interest as it allows us to define reading practices of the owner of the books who comissioned a bookbinder to bind them specifically together. It means it had meaning for the owner ! Great job, in so many crafts, you are an inspiration !

It would be easy to make a stand that would make a book like this easily readable. You would just need a baseplate as large as the open book, and two smaller platform plates to support the covers, which can be raised and lowered along vertical guide posts to the necessary heights. Imagine two hands holding a slinky, and you'll get the idea of the mechanics behind it. The whole thing could be nicely made from wood. For bonus points, you could build a stand that uses two pulleys attached to two center posts, at the top and bottom positions of the book spine, with cables that automatically raise and lower the two platforms as the pages are turned, keeping further to the "wheel" theme.
